<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A high-quality reference genome of wild Cannabis sativa"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-05-02"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Cannabis sativa is a well-known plant species that has great economic and ecological significance. An incomplete genome of cloned C. sativa was obtained by using SOAPdenovo software in 2011. To further explore the utilization of this plant resource, we generated an updated draft genome sequence for wild-type varieties of C. sativa in China using PacBio single-molecule sequencing and Hi-C technology. Our assembled genome is approximately 808&#8201;Mb, with scaffold and contig N50 sizes of 83.00&#8201;Mb and 513.57&#8201;kb, respectively. Repetitive elements account for 74.75% of the genome. A total of 38,828 protein-coding genes were annotated, 98.20% of which were functionally annotated. We provide the first comprehensive de novo genome of wild-type varieties of C. sativa distributed in Tibet, China. Due to long-term growth in the wild environment, these varieties exhibit higher heterozygosity and contain more genetic information. This genetic resource is of great value for future investigations of cannabinoid metabolic pathways and will aid in promoting the commercial production of C. sativa and the effective utilization of cannabinoids. The assembled genome is also a valuable resource for intensively and effectively investigating the C. sativa genome further in the future."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Cannabis sativa</i> is a well-known plant species that has great economic and ecological significance. An incomplete genome of cloned <i>C. sativa</i> was obtained by using SOAPdenovo software in 2011. To further explore the utilization of this plant resource, we generated an updated draft genome sequence for wild-type varieties of <i>C. sativa</i> in China using PacBio single-molecule sequencing and Hi-C technology. Our assembled genome is approximately 808âMb, with scaffold and contig N50 sizes of 83.00âMb and 513.57âkb, respectively. Repetitive elements account for 74.75% of the genome. A total of 38,828 protein-coding genes were annotated, 98.20% of which were functionally annotated. We provide the first comprehensive <i>de novo</i> genome of wild-type varieties of <i>C. sativa</i> distributed in Tibet, China. Due to long-term growth in the wild environment, these varieties exhibit higher heterozygosity and contain more genetic information. This genetic resource is of great value for future investigations of cannabinoid metabolic pathways and will aid in promoting the commercial production of <i>C. sativa</i> and the effective utilization of cannabinoids. The assembled genome is also a valuable resource for intensively and effectively investigating the <i>C. sativa</i> genome further in the future.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p><i>Cannabis sativa</i> L., a native plant of Central Asia, is first cultivated in Asia and Europe and is now one of the most popularly cultivated plants worldwide<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Schultes, R. E., Klein, W. M., Plowman, T. &amp; Lockwood, T. E. Cannabis: an example of taxonomic neglect. Bot. Mus. Leafl., Harv. Univ. 23, 337â367 (1974)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d66625e457">1</a></sup>. In China, hemp fiber has been used to produce textiles for the past 6000 years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Li, H.-L. An archaeological and historical account of cannabis in China. Econ. Bot. 28, 437â448 (1973)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d66625e461">2</a></sup>.</p><p><i>C. sativa</i> is one of the most valuable agriculturally important crops in nature. Although it is widely used to produce paper, textiles, building materials, food, and medicine, a secondary metabolite, tetrahydrocannabinol (THC), is also used to produce well-known drugs. Frequent, long-term, selective breeding has produced both hemp fiber and medicinal cannabis strains, with medicinal cannabis showing promise in effectively treating various diseases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Leung, L. Cannabis and its derivatives: review of medical use. J. Am. Board Fam. Med. 24, 452â462 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d66625e470">3</a></sup> by relieving an array of symptoms, including pain, nausea, anxiety, and inflammation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ruiz, L., Miguel, A. &amp; DÄ±Ìaz-Laviada, I. Î9âTetrahydrocannabinol induces apoptosis in human prostate PCâ3 cells via a receptorâindependent mechanism. FEBS Lett. 458, 400â404 (1999)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d66625e474">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Esposito, G., De Filippis, D., Carnuccio, R., Izzo, A. A. &amp; Iuvone, T. The marijuana component cannabidiol inhibits Î²-amyloid-induced tau protein hyperphosphorylation through Wnt/Î²-catenin pathway rescue in PC12 cells. J. Mol. Med. 84, 253â258 (2006)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d66625e474_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="MartÃ­n-Moreno, A. M. et al. Cannabidiol and other cannabinoids reduce microglial activation in vitro and in vivo: relevance to Alzheimersâ² disease. Mol. Pharmacol. 79, 964â973 (2011)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d66625e474_2">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Steffens, S. et al. Low dose oral cannabinoid therapy reduces progression of atherosclerosis in mice. Nature 434, 782 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d66625e477">7</a></sup>. The therapeutic efficacy of medicinal cannabis is mainly dependent on cannabinoids, which are endemic metabolites unique to <i>C. sativa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Taura, F., Sirikantaramas, S., Shoyama, Y., Shoyama, Y. &amp; Morimoto, S. Phytocannabinoids in Cannabis sativa: recent studies on biosynthetic enzymes. Chem. Biodivers. 4, 1649â1663 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d66625e483">8</a></sup>, among which THC and cannabidiol (CBD) are the main chemical cannabinoid compounds.</p><p>Although cannabis has considerable economic and medical value, information about its genome is limited. While a genomic draft was published recently, in 2011<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Van Bakel, H. et al. The draft genome and transcriptome of Cannabis sativa. Genome Biol. 12, R102 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d66625e490">9</a></sup>, the splicing of this draft was neither of good quality nor complete, thus hindering its usefulness.</p><p>Cannabis is mostly dioecious, with a diploid genome (2<i>n</i>â=â20) containing nine pairs of autosomes and one pair of sex chromosomes (female plants (XX) and male plants (XY)). The Y chromosome is larger than the X chromosome, and the female plantâs haploid genome is estimated to be 818âMb in size, while the male plantâs genome is estimated to be 843âMb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ming, R., Bendahmane, A. &amp; Renner, S. S. Sex chromosomes in land plants. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 62, 485â514 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d66625e500">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Sakamoto, K., Akiyama, Y., Fukui, K., Kamada, H. &amp; Satoh, S. Characterization; genome sizes and morphology of sex chromosomes in hemp (Cannabis sativa L.). Cytologia 63, 459â464 (1998)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d66625e503">11</a></sup>. However, the published genome is for cloned <i>C. sativa</i> and was assembled using SOAPdenovo software to obtain a genome of approximately 786âMb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Van Bakel, H. et al. The draft genome and transcriptome of Cannabis sativa. Genome Biol. 12, R102 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d66625e510">9</a></sup>. It shows a contig N50â=â2.8âkb and scaffold N50â=â16.2âkb, and genome annotations are missing. Additionally, the genome assembly quality is poor since it contains incomplete assembly of gene regions and repeat sequences.</p><p>The cannabis genome has been sequenced<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Van Bakel, H. et al. The draft genome and transcriptome of Cannabis sativa. Genome Biol. 12, R102 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d66625e518">9</a></sup>, but the sequenced plant came from a cultivated variety. Generally, cultivated varieties lose substantial genetic diversity through successive bottlenecks due to domestication and selection for traits to increase yield under intensive human cultivation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Li, Y. H. et al. De novo assembly of soybean wild relatives for pan-genome analysis of diversity and agronomic traits. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1045â1052 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d66625e522">12</a></sup>. Therefore, wild-type varieties are an important source of genetic diversity for molecular breeding. In this report, we performed genomic sequencing, assembly, annotation, and evolutionary analysis in wild-type varieties of <i>C. sativa</i>. The genetic data obtained in this study will be a valuable resource for future studies assessing the pharmacology, chemical constituents, cultivation, and genetic improvement of the traits of these plants and could be used as a reference in future population genetic studies of <i>C. sativa</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sample collection and sequencing</h3><p>One female of the wild-type <i>C. sativa</i> âJLâ variety was used for whole-genome sequencing in this study. The sequencing depth was 153Ã, and 124âGb of genomic data were obtained. The subread N50 was 13.5âkb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#Tab1">1</a>). Additionally, we performed next-generation, paired-end sequencing and finally obtained a total of 95.97âGb of clean data. These data were then used to evaluate the quality of the wild-type <i>C. sativa</i> âJLâ variety genome.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistical results for PacBio sequencing data</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>RNA sequencing data were used for genome function annotation. Using paired-end sequencing, we obtained 42.1âGb of clean data, with each sample producing 6.5â9.8âGb (Supplementary Information; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome assembly</h3><p>A variety of methods were used for genome assembly, and the initial assembly yielded a genome size of 811,814,330âbp, with a contig N50 of 632,748âbp. After assembling the third-generation subreads, the next-generation data were used to correct the genome map. For this purpose, we used BWA (v0.7.9a, RRID: SCR 010910) to compare the next-generation clean reads with the assembled sequence, and based on the comparison results, we corrected the sequence using Pilon (v1.22, Broad Institute, MA, USA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS One 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d66625e617">13</a></sup>. The post-correction genome size was 812,295,151âbp, with a contig N50 of 633,146âbp. Statistical analysis of base pairs in the corrected genome showed that the average GC content in the genome was 33.8%. The contents of other base pairs are presented in Supplementary Information; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S2</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Hi-C</h3><p>The Hi-C approach efficiently uses high-throughput sequencing to determine the state of genome folding by measuring the frequency of contact between pairs of loci<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289â293 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d66625e632">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Rao, S. S. et al. A 3D map of the human genome at kilobase resolution reveals principles of chromatin looping. Cell 159, 1665â1680 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d66625e635">15</a></sup>. Originally, this technique was developed to generate chromosomal genome assemblies, but it was subsequently found to be useful for genome-wide chromosome conformation capture<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Belaghzal, H., Dekker, J. &amp; Gibcus, J. H. Hi-C 2.0: An optimized Hi-C procedure for high-resolution genome-wide mapping of chromosome conformation. Methods 123, 56â65 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d66625e639">16</a></sup>.</p><p>Nearly 487 million raw reads (146.25âGb) were collected and then reduced to 424 million clean reads after filtering out low-quality reads and retaining reads with more than 5% N bases, adapter reads, and single reads. We then successfully clustered, ordered and oriented 2,506 contigs into 10 groups according to the agglomerative hierarchical clustering method in Lachesis (<a href="https://github.com/shendurelab/LACHESIS">https://github.com/shendurelab/LACHESIS</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119â1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d66625e650">17</a></sup>, representing 91% and 99% of the total genome by contig number and base count, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#Tab2">2</a>). Along with Hi-C analysis, we visually inspected contig orientation and suspicious fragments and found little incorrect information, and we identified and corrected that information using self-written scripts. We obtained a high-quality chromosome-level cannabis genome with a contig N50 of 513âkb and a scaffold N50 of 83âMb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#Tab3">3</a>). According to a heatmap of the contig contact matrix with Hi-C data (Supplementary Information; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S1</a>), we estimated that the clustering, ordering, and orientation of the contigs was valid (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#Tab4">4</a>). Among these contigs, the scaffold N50 was 162 times greater than the scaffold N50 of the preliminarily assembled genes. There were 2,506 mounted and 245 unmounted scaffolds on the chromosomes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#Tab5">5</a>). Our <i>C. sativa</i> genome is a solidly based genomic resource for cultivar identification, population analysis, and functional analysis.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Summary of contig/scaffold clustering results</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Summary of Hi-C auxiliary assembly results</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-4"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab4" data-test="table-caption">Table 4 Contig/scaffold sorting results</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3/tables/4"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-5"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab5" data-test="table-caption">Table 5 Summary of Hi-C-assisted assembly pseudomolecule lengths</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3/tables/5"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Assessment of genomic integrity</h3><p>BUSCO (v3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d66625e996">18</a></sup> was employed to evaluate the accuracy and completeness of our genome assembly, gene set, and transcripts. Based on the OrthoDB (<a href="http://cegg.unige.ch/orthodb">http://cegg.unige.ch/orthodb</a>) database, BUSCO built several large, single-copy gene sets covering the branches of the evolutionary tree. When comparing the gene set to the genome, it was noted that the proportion of complete BUSCOs was 92.6% (Supplementary Information; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S3</a>), indicating that the genome assembly integrity was very good.</p><p>Due to potential contamination during sequencing and assembly, we further evaluated our genome assembly by using GC depth analysis. The GC depth scatter plot showed no significant differentiation, and points were concentrated around the 34% area, indicating high assembly quality without any bacterial contamination (Supplementary Information; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S2</a>). Finally, the sequencing profile base depth was close to a Poisson distribution, further indicating that the assembled genome showed high assembly quality (Supplementary Information; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S3</a>).</p><p>To evaluate the consistency of the next-generation data, we compared the sequencing reads to the assembled scaffold sequences, and the resultant comparison ratio for the reads and genomic coverage showed that they were deep and complete (Supplementary Information; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S4</a>). The comparison rate of the next-generation data was 96.77% (Supplementary Information; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S4</a>), indicating that the assembled genome was of high quality.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genome annotation</h3><p>Repeat sequences, including tandem repeats and interspersed repeats, are important components in the genome, and there are two strategies for predicting such sequences. The total lengths of transposon elements obtained from the genome sequence were 118,700,582 and 161,847,743âbp, representing 14.61% and 19.92% of the genome, respectively. For <i>de novo</i> prediction, RepeatModeler (v1.0.8) was used to first establish a <i>de novo</i> repeat sequence library, after which the genome sequence was predicted using RepeatMasker (v4.0.6). These results revealed a total length of 584,319,477âbp, representing 71.93% of the genome. In addition, we used the <i>de novo</i> prediction method with Tandem Repeat Finder (v4.09, <a href="https://tandem.bu.edu/trf/trf.html">https://tandem.bu.edu/trf/trf.html</a>) to identify tandem repeats in the genome. The total length obtained by this method was 22,382,718âbp, representing 2.76% of the genome. Finally, we removed overlapping portions of the non-redundant repeats that we identified with those procedures, resulting in 612,733,451âbp of non-redundant repeats, which accounted for 74.75% of the assembled genome (Supplementary Information; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S5</a>; Figshare 1). Long terminal repeats (LTRs) represented 50% of the repeat sequences in the assembled genome (Supplementary Information; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S6</a>).</p><p>After predicting the gene approval rating, we used CPC (v0.9-r2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Kong, L. et al. CPC: assess the protein-coding potential of transcripts using sequence features and support vector machine. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W345âW349 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1057">19</a></sup> to analyze the potential of the predicted genes and to generate 38,828 predicted genes. The average length of the predicted genes was 551.10âbp, and the average length of their coding sequences was 1153.47âbp. There were an average of four exons per gene with a length of 281.91âbp per exon, and the average intron length was 2147.77âbp. After comparing our predicted gene set with the functional databases SwissProt, NT (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nucleotide/">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nucleotide/</a>), NR, PFAM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Finn, R. D. et al. Pfam: the protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D222âD230 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1065">20</a></sup>, eggNOG (<a href="http://eggnogdb.embl.de/">http://eggnogdb.embl.de/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Powell, S. et al. eggNOG v3.0: orthologous groups covering 1133 organisms at 41 different taxonomic ranges. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D284âD289 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1073">21</a></sup>, GO (<a href="http://geneontology.org/page/go-database">http://geneontology.org/page/go-database</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. The Gene Ontology Consortium. Nat. Genet. 25, 25â29 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1082">22</a></sup>, and KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Kanehisa, M. Molecular network analysis of diseases and drugs in KEGG. Methods Mol. Biol. 939, 263â275 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1086">23</a></sup>, 38,129 genes were annotated, accounting for 98.20% of the genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#Tab6">6</a>, Figshare 2). These results were statistically graphed in the NT, NR, UniProt-BLASTX, and UniProt-BLASTP databases (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#Fig1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-6"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab6" data-test="table-caption">Table 6 Summary of gene function annotations</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3/tables/6"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Gene annotation Venn diagram."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Gene annotation Venn diagram.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure"
