/></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Comparison of the annotated gene results from the NT, NR, UniProt-BLASTX, and UniProt-BLASTP databases</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We predicted non-coding RNAs, such as rRNAs, tRNAs, snRNAs, and miRNAs, by comparing their sequences with the known non-coding RNA library Rfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Griffiths-Jones, S. et al. Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, D121âD124 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1203">24</a></sup>. A total of 2,441 rRNAs, 214 snRNAs, and 281 miRNAs were thus predicted (Supplementary Information; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S7</a>, Figshare 3, 4, 5). tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955â964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1210">25</a></sup> was used to predict tRNA sequences in the genome, resulting in 712 tRNAs (Supplementary Information; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S7</a>). To further verify our gene annotation results, we conducted a BUSCO evaluation using the embryophyta_odb10 database, producing a result of 93%, indicating that the annotation results were acceptable (Supplementary Information; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Gene families and phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>OrthoMCL (v1.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1228">26</a></sup> was used to classify gene families with single and multiple copies from both closely related and remotely related species. (Supplementary Information; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S9</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S5</a>), resulting in the identification of 930 <i>C. sativa</i>-specific genes. <i>C. sativa</i> shows more genes in common with <i>Trema orientale</i> and <i>Morus notabilis</i> than with other species (Supplementary Information; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S6</a>). We used MUSCLE software (v3.8.31)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1254">27</a></sup> to perform multiple sequence alignments for all single-copy gene families sequences. After we constructed the integrated supergene sequence, which was based on the four-fold degenerated sites (4DTv sites) of orthologous family genes, we used PhyML (v3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Guindon, S. et al. New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst. Biol. 59, 307â321 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1258">28</a></sup> to construct the species phylogenetic tree (ML-Tree). As shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#Fig2">2</a>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i> and <i>Fragaria vesca</i> in Rosaceae diverged from one another earlier than <i>T. orientale</i>, <i>M. notabilis</i>, and <i>Ziziphus jujuba</i> diverged from each other, and <i>C. sativa</i> is most closely related to <i>T. orientale</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="C. sativa phylogenetic tree."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: <i>C. sativa</i> phylogenetic tree.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0295-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_295_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0295-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_295_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="324" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The longer the branch length, the longer the divergence time. The closer the branches, the closer the predicted genetic relationship. In general, we considered a bootstrapping value above 85 to represent good support for the result. Numbers above the nodes are bootstrap values</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Based on our phylogenetic analysis of the integrated supergene sequence, we used PAMLâs MCMCTREE software (v4.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1302">29</a></sup> and the Bayesian relaxed molecular clock method to estimate divergence time. We used corrected time directly obtained from TimeTree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G., Suleski, M. &amp; Hedges, S. B. TimeTree: a resource for timelines, timetrees, and divergence times. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 1812â1819 (2017)." href="#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1306">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zeng, Q. et al. Definition of eight mulberry species in the genus morus by internal transcribed spacer-based phylogeny. PLoS One 10, e0135411 (2015)." href="#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1306_1">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Foster, C. S. P. et al. Evaluating the impact of genomic data and priors on Bayesian estimates of the angiosperm evolutionary timescale. Syst. Biol. 66, 338â351 (2017)." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1306_2">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Massoni, J., Couvreur, T. L. &amp; Sauquet, H. Five major shifts of diversification through the long evolutionary history of Magnoliidae (angiosperms). BMC Evolut. Biol. 15, 49 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1309">33</a></sup> (see Supplementary Material <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM10">10</a>). The divergence time corresponding to the crown clade of Eudicots was 115âMa (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#Fig3">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Phylogenetic relationships and divergence time."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Phylogenetic relationships and divergence time.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0295-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_295_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0295-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_295_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="364" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The blue numbers at the node positions represent the divergence time of each species in millions of years (Ma). The numbers in parentheses indicate the confidence interval of the divergence time, which can be used to estimate the divergence time of target species and other species. The red dots are the calibration time used to correct the time of species divergence</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Whole-genome duplication and gene families expansion/contraction analysis</h3><p>Gene families expansion and contraction were analyzed based on mathematical statistical tests. After the cluster analysis of gene families, those with abnormal gene numbers in individual species were filtered, and CAFE (v4.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="De Bie, T., Cristianini, N., Demuth, J. P. &amp; Hahn, M. W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269â1271 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1335">34</a></sup> and probabilistic graphical models (PGMs) were then used to simulate the acquisition and loss of genes under the specified phylogenetic tree and to analyze gene families expansion and contraction using hypothesis testing (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#Fig4">4</a>). We found 12,801 gene families in the MCRA (most recent common ancestor). In comparison to <i>M. notabilis</i>, <i>T. orientale, V. vinifera, F. vesca</i>, <i>Musca domestica</i>, <i>Z. jujuba</i>, and <i>Papaver somniferum</i>, there were 2,599 gene families showing expansion and 1,298 gene families showing contraction in <i>C. sativa</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fourfold degenerate site (4DTv) value distribution."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Fourfold degenerate site (4DTv) value distribution.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0295-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_295_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0295-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_295_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="391" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Green numbers represent the number of gene families present when a species expanded during evolution, and red numbers represent the number of gene families present when a species contracted during evolution</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Whole-genome duplication events are widespread in plants and are a potent force that drives plant genome evolution. We used MCscan (v.0.8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275â1281 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1372">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1375">36</a></sup> to identify genome synteny blocks within <i>C. sativa</i> and other related species. Using MUSCLE (v3.8.31)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1382">27</a></sup>, we performed multiple sequence alignment of the internal sequences of the blocks and then calculated 4DTv site values. Based on the abundance of 4DTv site values, we estimated the relative timing of whole-genome duplication (WGD) or species split events. In general, three significant peaks were seen in the <i>C. sativa</i> genome (4DTv ~0.19, ~0,42, and ~0.92; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#Fig5">5</a>), suggesting that <i>C. sativa</i> has experienced three WGD events. We also identified the 4DTv values from collinear blocks between <i>C. sativa</i> and the genomes of three closely related species, <i>M. notabilis</i>, <i>T. orientale</i>, and <i>Z. jujuba</i>. Additionally, we identified two ancient WGD events in <i>T. orientale</i> with two peaks at ~0.58 and 1.0. The Î³ event occurred after the divergence between monocots and dicots approximately 185âÂ±â55 million years ago (Mya)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467, (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1412">37</a></sup>. Therefore, this pattern indicates that <i>C. sativa</i> experienced large-scale gene duplication more recently than <i>T. orientale</i>, 35âMya. The divergence of <i>C. sativa</i> and <i>T. orientale</i> occurred at ~52âMya (4DTv ~0.28), and <i>C. sativa</i> and <i>T. orientale</i> shared two WGD events (4DTv ~0.42 to ~0.58 and ~0.92 to ~1.0).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="4DTv."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: 4DTv.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0295-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_295_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0295-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_295_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="539" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>The abscissa represents the 4DTv value, and the ordinate represents the proportion of genes corresponding to the 4DTv values</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Genomic synteny block analysis can be used to determine the evolutionary source of chromosomes between species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486â488 (2008)." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1446">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Soderlund, C., Bomhoff, M. &amp; Nelson, W. M. SyMAP v3.4: a turnkey synteny system with application to plant genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, e68 (2011)." href="#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1446_1">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Dujon, B. et al. Genome evolution in yeasts. Nature 430, 35â44 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1449">40</a></sup>. In this study, we used BLASTP (v2.2.31+) to analyze the aligned protein sequences of <i>C. sativa</i> and <i>Z. jujuba</i> (Rhamnaceae) and then used MCScan (v0.8) to evaluate those results by using genome synteny blocks. Our results showed that <i>C. sativa</i> and <i>Z. jujuba</i> present a strong genomic synteny relationship (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#Fig6">6a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Synteny analysis and LTR analysis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Synteny analysis and LTR analysis.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0295-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_295_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0295-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_295_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="894" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b> <i>C. sativa</i> and <i>Z. jujuba</i> synteny showing the span of their shared regions. <b>b</b> Long terminal repeat (LTR) occurrence time in different species</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>LTR recognition and the identification of occurrence history are important in plant genome research. In a plant genome, LTR retrotransposons are the type of transposon constituting the majority of plant TEs. The identification of the historical occurrence of such elements is important for plant genome research, thus placing much emphasis on characterizing LTR structure and identifying LTR sequences. At the same time, the study of LTR evolution elucidates the evolution of plant genome structure and function. LTRs are involved in shaping genomic structure and size, thus affecting the regulation and variation of genes and the origin of new genes. Since the discovery of LTR retrotransposons, research has shown that differences in LTR occurrence can be identified by comparing the time difference between sequenced, target species and related species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Shi, J. et al. Chromosome conformation capture resolved near complete genome assembly of broomcorn millet. Nature 10, 464 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1492">41</a></sup>. In this study, LTRharvest (v1.5.10)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinform. 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1496">42</a></sup> and LTR_Finder (v1.0.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1500">43</a></sup> were used to identify <i>de novo</i> LTR regions of <i>C. sativa</i>, <i>T. orientale</i>, and <i>Z. jujuba</i>. LTR_retriever (<a href="https://github.com/oushujun/LTR_retriever">https://github.com/oushujun/LTR_retriever</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="OuS. &amp; JiangN. LTR_retriever: a highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of long terminal repeat retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 176, 1410â1422 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1521">44</a></sup> was used to integrate the results from LTRharvest and LTR_Finder to obtain a high-quality LTR-RT library and to perform genome-wide LTR-RT annotation. We then generated LTR annotation regions and sequence information for the three species (Supplementary Information; Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S11</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#MOESM1">S13</a>). Through this LTR recognition, we found that the LTR retrotransposon content of 37.1% constituted most of the LTR TEs. Our estimation of LTR occurrence times revealed that <i>C. sativa</i>âs recent LTR burst differed from those of both <i>T. orientale</i> and <i>Z. jujuba</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#Fig6">6b</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p><i>C. sativa</i> is an invaluable plant species given its economic and ecological significance. Here, we generated an updated draft genome for wild-type varieties of <i>C. sativa</i> in China using PacBio single-molecule sequencing and Hi-C technology. Our assembled genome is approximately 808âMb, with scaffold and contig N50 sizes of 83.00âMb and 513.57âkb, respectively. Our new genome version is more contiguous than the previously assembled genome according to its a contig N50 of 12âkb. Therefore, the genome assembled in this study is superior to the previously assembled genome version.</p><p>In this study, we found a contig N50 of 632âkb for the PacBio read assembly. The genome continued to be assembled with the help of the Hi-C physical map, and the contig N50 became 513âkb. Here, we assumed that Hi-C technology concatenated the contigs into superlong scaffolds at the chromosome level. If the contigs were initially assembled correctly, the contig N50 would not change. However, due to the high heterozygosity of the wild-type varieties of <i>C. sativa</i>, chimeras were prone to occur during the assembly process, so the result of the assembly would be cut. Once the contig was cut, the contig N50 would decrease slightly, and we therefore considered the slight decrease in the contig N50 to be normal. Scaffolding with Hi-C further facilitated the assignment of all scaffolds to chromosomal positions. In this study, the new genome assembly presented higher genome contiguity and sequence quality than the previous genome assembly.</p><p>Because of the high repetition rate and high heterozygosity in the cannabis genome, no high-quality cannabis genome has been generated previously. There are unknown regions in the cannabis genome assembled using SOAPdenovo software by Van Bakel et al. in 2011<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Van Bakel, H. et al. The draft genome and transcriptome of Cannabis sativa. Genome Biol. 12, R102 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1567">9</a></sup>. The genome was not assembled to the chromosome level, and the number and length of the scaffolds in that study are much lower than the values expected for plant genes. Therefore, the cannabis genome assembled in 2011 shows poor quality and does not contain annotation information, which greatly limits its applicability to research on cannabis. In this study, we reassembled a wild-type cannabis genome by using third-generation sequencing data and thus obtained a high-quality cannabis genome.</p><p>Single-molecule real-time sequencing has the characteristics of a high throughput and long read length, which can reduce the number of contigs after sequencing and can effectively increase the number and integrity of genomes during the process of genome splicing. We combined TGS and NGS sequencing methods with Hi-C assembly technology to construct a high-density wild-type cannabis genome sequence map.</p><p>After obtaining the high-quality cannabis genome, we annotated its genes and thus considerably improved upon the 2011<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Van Bakel, H. et al. The draft genome and transcriptome of Cannabis sativa. Genome Biol. 12, R102 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1578">9</a></sup> version of the genome. Following the completion of the assembly of its repeat sequences and statistical analysis, we found that cannabis has abundant repeat regions, which may be the cause of the poor quality of the cannabis genome assembled by Van Bakel et al. in 2011<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Van Bakel, H. et al. The draft genome and transcriptome of Cannabis sativa. Genome Biol. 12, R102 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1582">9</a></sup>. This high-quality reference genome will undoubtedly benefit researchers in the exploration and manipulation of the agronomic characteristics of <i>C. sativa</i>.</p><p>To understand the evolutionary status of cannabis, we analyzed its evolution and divergence times. Through these analysis, we found that the evolutionary status of cannabis and <i>T. orientale</i> is close at the molecular level, and their kinship is thus very close. The quality of the <i>T. orientale</i> genome is still relatively poor, and the high-quality cannabis genome that we obtained in this study could therefore provide useful information for the future study of the <i>T. orientale</i> genome and its evolution. By analyzing whole-genome duplication events in cannabis, we found three recent WGD events and one large-scale duplication event in cannabis and that cannabis shares two WGD incidents with <i>T. orientale</i>. Our data further elucidate the evolutionary status of cannabis.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">DNA extraction and sequencing</h3><p>All samples were collected from the Kyirong Gully (28Â°28â²â28Â°66â² N, 85Â°13â²â85Â°28â² E) in Tibet, China. The Kyirong Gully is a plateau gorge with an altitude of 1,700â6,000âm located on the south slope of the Himalayas and is very isolated from the outside world. Its special geography and environment make the gorge a typical vertical ecological system of Himalayan areas, and it is considered âthe richest species gene bank in a plateau areaâ. We extracted high-quality genomic DNA from the leaves of female <i>C. sativa</i> using a Plant DNA kit (TIANGEN Biotech, Beijing, China). A 10â20âkb SMRTbell DNA library (Pacific Biosciences of California, CA, USA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific Biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS One 7, e47768 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1619">45</a></sup> was prepared using BluePippin for DNA size selection (Sage Science, MA, USA) and then sequenced on the PacBio Sequel platform (Pacific Biosciences of California, CA, USA) with P6/C4 chemistry. Finally, 124âGb of subreads were obtained, with 153Ã coverage of the <i>C. sativa</i> genome.</p><p>A paired-end library with an insert size of 350âbp was constructed and sequenced using the Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform.</p><p>Total RNA was extracted from the roots, stems, leaves, and seeds using the QIAGEN Total RNA Extraction Kit (QIAGEN, Hilden, Germany), and RNA-seq libraries were then constructed using the TruSeq RNA Library Kit (Illumina, CA, USA). These data were used for gene structure prediction.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Genome assembly</h3><p>Since PacBio single-molecule sequencing usually shows an unacceptably high error rate, we used Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722â736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1640">46</a></sup> (v1.5) to perform self-correction before assembly. A variety of long-read assemblers, such as SMARTdenovo (v1.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Istace, B. et al. De novo assembly and population genomic survey of natural yeast isolates with the Oxford Nanopore MinION sequencer. GigaScience 6, 1â13 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1644">47</a></sup> and Wtdgb2 (v2.1), were employed for <i>C. sativa</i> contig construction using the corrected subreads. To further improve contig continuity, the QuickMerge meta-assembler (<a href="https://github.com/mahulchak/quickmerge">https://github.com/mahulchak/quickmerge</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Chakraborty, M., Baldwin-Brown, J. G., Long, A. D. &amp; Emerson, J. J. Contiguous and accurate de novo assembly of metazoan genomes with modest long read coverage. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, e147 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1655">48</a></sup> was employed with the contigs from Wtdbg2 as the query input and those from SMARTdenovo (v1.5) as the ref input. The two sets of contigs were aligned using NUCmer from MUMmer (v4.0.0) with the parameters â-l 100â and the delta-filter parameters â-i 95 -râ. Then, QuickMerge (<a href="https://github.com/mahulchak/quickmerge">https://github.com/mahulchak/quickmerge</a>) was used with the parameters â-hco 5.0 -c 1.5 -lm 5000â. The errors in the primary assembly were corrected by using PacBio subreads with blasr (v5.1) and Arrow (v2.2.1), and the Illumina paired-end reads were then mapped to the contigs using bwa-mem to polish the contigs with Pilon (v1.22, Broad Institute, MA, USA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS One 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1664">13</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Evaluation of the assembled genome</h3><p>Two methods were used to evaluate the quality of the final assembly: (1) We used the Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO v3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1676">18</a></sup> approach to evaluate the accuracy and completeness of the genome assembly, which provides quantitative measures for the assessment of genome assembly based on evolutionarily informed expectations of gene content from near-universal single-copy orthologs; and (2) Illumina paired-end reads were mapped to the final assembly to evaluate its completeness using bwa-mem with the default parameters.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Chromosome assembly using Hi-C</h3><p>In this study, we used <i>C. sativa</i> root samples for Hi-C experiments and data analysis, and leaf samples from the same plant were used for to generate a Hi-C library. The Hi-C library was prepared using a NEBNext Ultra II DNA Library Prep Kit, and the library was sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform.</p><p>The clean reads were mapped to the <i>C. sativa</i> genome using Bowtie2 (v2.2.3) with the default parameters. The two ends of paired reads were separately mapped to the genome. After filtering out dangling ends, self-annealing sequences, and dumped pairs, the valid paired-end reads of unique, mapped paired-end reads were collected using HiC-Pro (v2.10)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Servant, N. et al. HiTC: exploration of high-throughput âCâ experiments. Bioinformatics 28, 2843â2844 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1697">49</a></sup>. Finally, we applied the agglomerative hierarchical clustering method in Lachesis (<a href="https://github.com/shendurelab/LACHESIS">https://github.com/shendurelab/LACHESIS</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119â1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1705">17</a></sup>. Valid interaction pairs were used to build interaction matrices and scale up the primary contigs to chromosome-scale scaffolds with LACHESIS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119â1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1709">17</a></sup>. The general procedure of LACHESIS is to first cluster the contigs into chromosomal groups with the agglomerative hierarchical clustering algorithm and then order and orient the contigs of each chromosomal group into pseudochromosomes. We set CLUSTER_N=10 for LACHESIS, and then performed full-range scanning of the five key parameters, including CLUSTER_MIN_RE_SITES [15, 2000], CLUSTER_MAX_LINK_DENSITY [1, 10], CLUSTER_NONINFORMATIVE_RATIO [1v, 10], ORDER_MIN_N_RES_IN_TRUNK [15, 2000], and ORDER_MIN_N_RES_IN_SHREDS [15, 2000]. After approximately 1,000 trials, the best candidate was selected if it included &gt;95% of the ordered contig length and rearranged manually. Finally, 2,506 contigs (representing 99.8% of the total length) were anchored to 10 pseudochromosomes of <i>C. sativa</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Assessment of genomic integrity</h3><p>We used BUSCO (v3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1726">18</a></sup> to evaluate the accuracy and completeness of our genome assembly, gene set, and transcripts. Based on the OrthoDB (<a href="http://cegg.unige.ch/orthodb">http://cegg.unige.ch/orthodb</a>) database, BUSCO builds several large, single-copy gene sets covering branches of the evolutionary tree. When comparing the gene set to the genome, we found that the proportion of complete BUSCOs was 92.6%, thus indicating very good genome assembly integrity.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Repeat element identification</h3><p>Repeat sequences, including tandem repeats and interspersed repeats, are important components of the genome. Two strategies, homology alignment and <i>de novo</i> searches, were combined to identify repeats. First, we identified homologous repeat sequences based on RepBase (<a href="https://www.girinst.org/server/RepBase/index.php">https://www.girinst.org/server/RepBase/index.php</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1749">50</a></sup>. In addition, we used the <i>de novo</i> prediction method of RepeatModeler (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/</a>) with the default parameters. Tandem Repeat Finder (<a href="https://tandem.bu.edu/trf/trf.html">https://tandem.bu.edu/trf/trf.html</a>) was used to find tandem repeats in the genome.</p><p>LTR-RTs are important for plant genome evolution, so we used LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinform. 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1768">42</a></sup> and LTR_Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1772">43</a></sup> to identify the <i>de novo</i> LTRs of <i>C. sativa</i>, <i>T. orientale</i> and <i>Z. jujuba</i>. LTR_retriever<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="OuS. &amp; JiangN. LTR_retriever: a highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of long terminal repeat retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 176, 1410â1422 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1789">44</a></sup> was used to integrate the results from LTRharvest and LTR_Finder to obtain a high-quality LTR-RT library and perform genome-wide LTR-RT annotation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Noncoding RNA prediction</h3><p>tRNA genes were detected with tRNAscan-SE (<a href="http://lowelab.ucsc.edu/tRNAscan-SE/">http://lowelab.ucsc.edu/tRNAscan-SE/</a>), and other noncoding RNAs, such as rRNAs, tRNAs, snRNAs, and miRNAs, were predicted by comparison with the known noncoding RNA library of the Rfam database (<a href="http://rfam.xfam.org/">http://rfam.xfam.org/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Gene prediction</h3><p>Gene models were predicted by integrating three approaches: homology-based, transcriptome-based, and ab initio prediction. In homology-based prediction, the protein sequences of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, and <i>Zea mays</i> were downloaded and aligned to the genome using BLAST (<i>E</i>-value: 1eâââ5), and gene models were defined using GeMoMa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Keilwagen, J. et al. Using intron position conservation for homology-based gene prediction. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, e89 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1829">51</a></sup>. CPC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Kong, L. et al. CPC: assess the protein-coding potential of transcripts using sequence features and support vector machine. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W345âW349 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1834">19</a></sup> was used to examine gene coding potential. A gene model was retained as the final set if it presented evidence of transcription and coding potential.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Functional annotation</h3><p>We predicted the function of the protein-coding gene set by searching against public databases including SwissProt (<a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/interpro/search/sequence-search">http://www.ebi.ac.uk/interpro/search/sequence-search</a>), NT (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nucleotide/">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nucleotide/</a>), NR (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/protein">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/protein/</a>), PFAM (<a href="http://xfam.org/">http://xfam.org/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Finn, R. D. et al. Pfam: the protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D222âD230 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1862">20</a></sup>, eggNOG (<a href="http://eggnogdb.embl.de">http://eggnogdb.embl.de</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Powell, S. et al. eggNOG v3.0: orthologous groups covering 1133 organisms at 41 different taxonomic ranges. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D284âD289 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1871">21</a></sup>, GO (<a href="http://geneontology.org/page/go-database">http://geneontology.org/page/go-database</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. The Gene Ontology Consortium. Nat. Genet. 25, 25â29 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1879">22</a></sup>, and KEGG (<a href="http://www.genome.jp/kegg/">http://www.genome.jp/kegg/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Kanehisa, M. Molecular network analysis of diseases and drugs in KEGG. Methods Mol. Biol. 939, 263â275 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1887">23</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">Gene families and phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>The genomes of <i>C. sativa</i> and seven other plants, including <i>Z. jujube, V. vinifera, F. vesca, P. somniferum, T. orientate, M. notabilis</i>, and <i>M. domestica</i>, were collected for evolutionary analysis. An all-vs.-all BLASTP (v2.2.28) (<i>E</i>-value: 1eâââ5) search was carried out, and then OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1911">26</a></sup> was used to identify paralogous and orthologous genes.</p><p>MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1918">27</a></sup> was used to perform multiple sequence alignments for all single-copy orthologous genes. After we constructed the integrated supergene sequence, which was based on the four-fold degenerate sites (4DTv sites) of single-copy orthologous genes, PhyML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Guindon, S. et al. New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst. Biol. 59, 307â321 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1922">28</a></sup> was used to construct the phylogenetic tree (ML-Tree) with bootstrap values. The divergence time between the eight species were estimated using MCMCTREE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1926">29</a></sup> of the PAML package. The calibration points were selected from the TimeTree database (<a href="http://www.timetree.org/">http://www.timetree.org/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G., Suleski, M. &amp; Hedges, S. B. TimeTree: a resource for timelines, timetrees, and divergence times. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 1812â1819 (2017)." href="#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1934">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zeng, Q. et al. Definition of eight mulberry species in the genus morus by internal transcribed spacer-based phylogeny. PLoS One 10, e0135411 (2015)." href="#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1934_1">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Foster, C. S. P. et al. Evaluating the impact of genomic data and priors on Bayesian estimates of the angiosperm evolutionary timescale. Syst. Biol. 66, 338â351 (2017)." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1934_2">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Massoni, J., Couvreur, T. L. &amp; Sauquet, H. Five major shifts of diversification through the long evolutionary history of Magnoliidae (angiosperms). BMC Evolut. Biol. 15, 49 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1937">33</a></sup> as the normal priors to restrain the age of the nodes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">Expansion/contraction analysis of gene families</h3><p>After gene families cluster analysis, the gene families sizes from OrthoMCL and the phylogenetic trees, including branch lengths, were used as inputs for CAFE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="De Bie, T., Cristianini, N., Demuth, J. P. &amp; Hahn, M. W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269â1271 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1950">34</a></sup>. The Î» value was estimated based on a stochastic birth and death process model. Gene families were considered significantly expanded or contracted when they presented <i>p</i> values smaller than 0.05.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec23">Synteny and whole-genome duplication</h3><p>Whole-genome duplication events are widespread in plants and are important for dynamic genome evolution. MCscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275â1281 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1965">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1968">36</a></sup> was used to identify synteny blocks, defined as regions with more than five collinear genes between paired genomes. MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0295-3#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d66625e1972">27</a></sup> was used to perform multiple sequence alignment for the sequences of the synteny blocks. The 4DTv value was calculated as the number of transversions at all four-fold degenerate synonymous sites.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The <i>C. sativa</i> genome project data were deposited at NCBI under BioProject number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA562042">PRJNA562042</a> and BioSamples SAMN12606152 and SAMN12855826. The DNAseq clean data, RNA-seq clean data, and Hi-C clean data of the wild Chinese <i>C. sativa</i> genome have been deposited in the Sequence Read Archive (SRA) database under accession numbers SRR10019825 and SRR10193436âSRR10193442. The <i>de novo</i> clean data of the wild <i>C. sativa</i> genome have been deposited in the Sequence Read Archive (SRA) database under accession numbers SRR10189115âSRR10189120. Detailed information on the genome can be found on the NCBI platform (<a href="https://dataview.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/object/PRJNA562042?reviewer=h2q2tfglbtejieim9o430aan6n">https://dataview.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/object/PRJNA562042?reviewer=h2q2tfglbtejieim9o430aan6n</a>). The data from this whole-genome project have been deposited at DDBJ/ENA/GenBank under accession number WRXK00000000. The version described in this paper is version WRXK01000000 under BioProject number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA562042">PRJNA562042</a> and BioSample SAMN12606152. All supplementary figures and tables are provided in the Supplementary Materials.</p>
            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="code-availability" data-title="Code availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="code-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="code-availability">Code availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="code-availability-content">
